7 Structures along Alley
Along the alley between Cartwright Field and the freeway several
structures are located within or near the edge of the zone of influence of the
tunnel The structures are one and two story apartments or single family
dwellings Most of the wood frame structures are supported on exposed wood
posts The two story structure has masonry block walls on shallow foun
dations
Protection measures should be carried out if settlements exceed 1 4 in
Compaction or replacement grouting techniques such as those used beneath the
freeway can be used Because the posts for the wood frame structure are
accessible it would also be possible to relevel the structures with the
owners assent if settlements were to exceed small values It is also
possible that controlled tunneling can be carried out to limit centerline
settlements to 1 in and keep building settlements to less than 1 4 in

Monitoring Procedures
Both groundwater and settlement monitoring should be conducted routinely
throughout the course of the project It is recommended that the contractor
be required to place settlement points and monitor settlements as the tunnel
passes beneath the settlement points The types of settlement points to be
astablished include
l centerline points 25 to 50 ft on center established at
the surface
2 shallow settlement points extending to a depth of
approximately 2 to 5 ft
3 deep settlement points with their anchor located
5 to 10 ft above tunnel crown
4 building points on corners of buildings within
100 ft of the tunnel centerline
Settlement cross sections will be established principally to monitor move
ments at and prior to reaching critical buildings highways and utilities
At least two sets of initial readings should be taken before the tunnel
aporoaches within 50 ft of the cross section of a given settlement point The
last reading should be taken within a few days prior to passage beneath the
settlement point to ensure that the point has not been disturbed Additional
readings should be taken if the initial readings show any significant
variations Readings obtained should be organized in a form which permits
comparison with previous readings and determination of total settlement and
changes in settlement Readings should be provided on a daily basis to the
project engineer In addition to the readings made by the contractor the
field forces of the project manager should carry out an independent survey
establishing the initial readings obtaining final readings and checking
intermediate readings

6 5 Tunnel Construction Considerations
General
Tunneling methods should be chosen that will be capable of advancing in
the soil and rock conditions encountered with ground movements controlled so
that excessive settlements are prevented Because of the variable nature of
conditions in sections of the tunnel it will be necessary to choose procedures
that are flexible able to adapt to changing soil or rock properties
Procedures that will allow control of the ground and support of the face will
be preferred to procedures that permit high speed tunneling in ideal con
ditions but are not suited for use in the difficult ground conditions
It is recommended that an open face shield be used for tunneling which
has capabilities for supporting the face as well as allowing access for remo
val of rock For short portions of the mixed face section of the project a
partial shield or no shield could be utilized but to do so will require
careful control of groundwater to prevent softening and failure of the invert
experienced tunnel personnel and the ability to support ahead of the face and
to breast the face
Because of the relatively short length of the tunnel and the variable
nature of the materials including the presence of running soils boulders
rock and cemented soils a wneel type excavator is not considered appropriate
The presence of large boulders and rock would also cause difficulties with
6 25
GEOLABS HAWAIll
pipe jacking Furthermore friction effects in the cinders are anticipated to
be high and will be difficult to reduce with bentonite because of the relati
vely high permeability of the cinders Thus pipe jacking lengths could be
short Therefore it is considered that pipe jacking is not appropriate for
this project
Tunneling Methods
The specifications require use of an opan face circular shield on
the western portion of the project Station 0 00 to 29 00
The open face shield should have the following capabilities capacity
to shove with muck in face ability to support face with muck capacity to
shove into cemented cinders ability to remove cemented soils in the face
ability to control runs and flows and prevent excessive ground losses even
when combinations of cemented and uncemented soils are present capable of
having the face fully breasted
Procedures should be employed to minimize the loss of ground as the
lining emerges from the tail by either grouting the tail void and or by
expanding the lining into the tail void If grout is chosen sufficient
volume should be made available at the end of the shove to permit filling of
the entire tail void which because of overbreak and ground loss can often
exceed the volume calculated from the annular space between the outside of the
lining and the outside of the shield
It is recommended that the contractor s submittal for his shield and
excavation procedure include a description of the capabilities for handling
the expected soil conditions
In much of the eastern portion of the project Station 29400 to
51 61 a mixed face of soil and rock will be encountered Tunneling is
required from Station 27 80 to 41 25 Hither tunneling or braced excavation
6 26
GEOLABS HAWAIli
may be used from Station 41 25 to An open excavation would probably
be most efficient in the rock at the far east end on Metcalf Street
An open face tunnel shield may be used in the tunnel section In addi
tion to having the characteristics noted for the western section of the pro
ject it should be capable of excavating large boulders rock layers
surrounded by soil or a full face of rock The basalt rock encountered in
the eastern portion of the project is hard The shield should be capable of
supporting running and flowing ground in combination with rock and boulders
In the process of excavating rock in the tunnel face it is likely that
overbreak extending beyond the perimeter of the shield will develop
Procedures should be carried out to fill this void around the shield or at
the tail before the surrounding soil displaces into the void causing large
loss of ground
From Station 29 00 to 51 61 in addition to a circular shield a par
tial shield in a circular or horseshoe cross section may be used or the
tunnel may be driven without a shield If a full circular shield is not used
extra care must be expended in order to maintain the stability and bearing
capacity of the bottom of the tunnel and prevent inflow of ground into the
tunnel Furthermore the methods must provide for full support of the tunnel
perimeter breasting of the entire face of the tunnel as required and abi
lity to spile or support the soils ahead of the face
Compaction grouting or replacement grouting should be employed over the
shield where large ground losses are occurring The procedure is required in
designated sections if movements reach or exceed limits established in the
specifications but is also to be used in areas where large subsidence would
otherwise occur
Mixed ground conditions such as those anticipated between Station 29 00
and 45 00 are some of the most difficult conditions to tunnel because of the
presence of flowing and or running ground combined with hard cobbles boulders
and rock One of the main difficulties lies in maintaining a stable face yet
providing access to the face to remove cobbles boulders and rock Conditions
6 27
GEOLABS HAWAIi
similar to those in Reaches 3 and 4 have caused abandonment of other tunnels
cause of alignment excavation support and or ground loss problems
Dewatering local treatment of the ground by consolidation grouting and
reinforcement of the material above the crown using rebars grouted in from the
ground surface may be needed to stabilize the soils in and above the tunnel
interval in Reaches 3 and 4
Dewatering wells should extend below tunnel invert and through permeable
zones To be effective a vacuum system will be required One potential
problem is complete or near complete dewatering of the wells and thus possible
damage or burnout of the pumps
Wells could be placed in two stages a wider spacing to locate and eva
luate water conditions and then intermediate wells placed based on the ini
tial well information
During tunneling replacement grout can be placed in zones where large
ground losses are occurring Throughout the project replacement grout should
be required if large ground losses develop beneath the streets utilities or
other facilities that cannot tolerate large settlements The void should be
filled prior to continuing the advance of the tunnel
It will be desirable to site the tunnel to provide as much accessible
Open space above the tunnel as possible in the mixed face sections in order to
minimize the effects of ground movements to provide access for installing
grout and dewatering holes and to provide areas where a braced excavation
could be placed to recover a shield or complete the installation of the pipe
should the contractor be unable to advance the tunnel

Punahou Street Crossing
1 Ground Conditions
At the Punahou Street crossing the excavated crown of the proposed
sewer will be located approximately 5 to 6 ft below the invert of the existing
line Materials encountered in and above the proposed sewer interval are
weakly cemented to uncemented cinder sand The sand is medium dense average
N value of 14 to 23 blows ft both in the tunnel interval and above the tunnel
crown see Boring No G 12 The groundwater level in the cinders is 1 to 2
ft above the invert A source of water may be from leakage from the existing
sewer Moreover methane gas was detected during drilling of Boring No G 12
Groundwater quantities are not expected to be a problem but special treatment
or discharge into the existing sewer may be needed to dispose of the con
taminated groundwater In addition the excavation may encounter explosive
gases Gas monitoring equipment for methane and hydrogen sulfide as well as
other safety precautions such as good ventilation will be needed
The cinders will exhibit either firm running or flowing behavior
depending on cementation and groundwater
Very stiff to hard silty clays and clayey silts with cobbles and
boulders are present at and 2 ft below sewer invert and are expacted in the
excavation However the upper 0 5 to 1 ft of the clay and silt is soft and
may cause the invert to be soft see Boring No G 11 Cobdbles and boulders
will tend to cause excavation problems in a tunnel
2 Existing Sewer
Based on the and Associates study 1971 the existing
sewer was cracked and contained voids behind the lining see Appendix K
Although repairs of 1973 were only reported for tunnel sections west of
6 30
GEOLABS HAWAII
Punahou Street our inspection of 1986 showed that patching of longitudinal
cracks had been carried out to a point several hundred ft east of Punahou
Street and visible open voids were not present Construction drawings 1929
indicate that the existing sewer is unreinforced and that the wall invert con
nection consists of a shear key that would allow vertical separation
Settlement of the invert could cause the slab to separate from the walls
3 Initial Diversion
Prior to excavating near the existing sewer a portion of the
existing sewer flow must be diverted and a conduit placed to carry the
remaining flow through or around the section
According to the Division of Wastewater Management the bypass capacity
that can be utilized is 24 mgd from Bypass Stations 2 and 3 Computer analy
sis Park Engineering Inc 1986 indicates that to achieve the 12 mgd
oyoass capacity at each of bypass Stations 2 and 3 would require placement of
some type of temporary flow obstruction at the bypass points to raise the
depth of flow to The analysis also indicates that the entrance capa
city can be increased to divert all of the flow at the bypass points as long
as the diverted flow does not exceed the downstream capacity of the total
bypass system Based on an estimated peak flow of 41 mgd in the Manoa Kaimuki
Interceptor the maximum remaining flow after bypassing 24 mgd would be 17
mgd Analysis indicates that a 30 in diameter pipe through the crossover
section would be satisfactory to handle the to contain a
maximum water height of approximately upstream of the pipe would pro
bably bé satisfactory The bulkheads should be located no closer than 75 ft
from the center of the crossover point and the conduit and pumping system
should be designed to carry the full 17 mgd discharge in the existing sewer
Emergency crews and energency equipment such as generators and extra pumos and
lines should be available at all times during the crossover work The line
snould be elevated to provide access to the invert
The existing sewer should be protected from damage during tunneling or
excavation and must be restored so that it is structurally sound and will not
leak prior to placing it back in service
6 31
GEOLABS HAWAII
The crossover section can be accomplished by either open cut or tun
neling or a combination of open cut and tunneling The approach for each
method is given in the following sections
4 Braced Cut Method
The sewer crossing at Punahou Street can be accomplished entirely
in a braced cut made from the ground surface The cut would have plan dimen
sions approximately 25 ft wide by 90 ft long Support would be provided using
soldier piles installed in predrilled holes to a depth of approximately 5 ft
below the proposed sewer invert and would be lagged with timber or shotecrete
and braced with struts extending across the excavations Care must be taken
to control drill hole alignment so that the existing sewer is not intersected
The open cut would be located principally in the westbound lanes of Wilder
Avenue and traffic control Use of decking over the cut would be needed
The braced cut would be excavated down to the existing tunnel The
existing tunnel would then be reinforced and supported Two alternatives then
would be possible 1 The excavation would be completed down to stable
material below the invert of the proposed sewer line The tunneling shield
would be walked from west to east through the open cut section and tunneling
would be resumed at the east end wall of the open cut The sewer pipe or cast
concrete lining of the proposed tunnel would be placed in the open trench
2 The excavation would not be continued below the existing sewer Rather
the tunnel would be driven in soil beneath the existing sewer tunnel which
would be underpinned and supported from the bracing
5 Tunnel Method
Construction of the crossover may also be carried out by tunneling
underneath the existing sewer However the risk is higher for causing damage
to the existing tunnel due to ground movements Catastrophic failure of the
existing tunnel and flooding of the proposed excavation should not occur
because the diversion will be in place prior to crossover construction
6 32
GEOLABS HAWAII
Prior to tunneling beneath the existing sewer an envelope of grout
would oe placed around the existing sewer tunnel and in the soils to be exca
vated in the proposed tunnel Initially a row of holes spaced 10 ft on
center extending to the tunnel crown should be placed over the centerline of
the existing sewer Cement grout should be placed to fill any open voids that
may still be present around the lining Grouting from holes drilled adjacent
to the tunnel would then be carried out to stabilize a soil zone extending
from near the invert of the proposed tunnel at the base of the cinder zone
up to an elevation near the crown of the existing sewer The zone should
extend approximately 8 ft from the centerline of the existing sewer over a
longitudinal distance of It is anticipated that 2 rows
of grout holes would be placed on each side of the tunnel with each row of
holes spaced approximately 5 ft on center Holes should be advanced using
wash techniques rather than percussion drilling Sleeve port pipes with ports
at 18 in maximum spacings should bs installed in the holes and grouted in
place with a weak cement bentonite grout Grouting would be carried out
through a single port at a time using packers inside the sleeve port casing
Grout to be injected into the ground shall consist of a mixture of cement and
sodium silicate Sodium silicate with organic reactants is not recommended
The outer row of holes would be grouted with set times designed to prevent the
grout from flowing more than 10 ft away from the centerline of the existing
tunnel The inner row of holes would be grouted after the outer row and
should be placed close enough to the existing sewer so that grout will
penetrate the soils located beneath the invert Initial stages of grouting
would be with cement and sodium silicate grouting of subsequent stages
alternate ports or interior rows of holes would be with micro fine cement
and sodium silicate Grout volumes must be carefully controlled Grout volu
mes and flow rates should be recorded for each port
It may also be feasible to grout from within the existing sewer tunnel
after the grouting from the surface is completed depending on the space
available and method of diversion of flow If grouting is carried out inside
the sewer tunnel the holes must be positively filled so that leakage will not
take place The grouting could be accomplished by drilling holes in the tan
nel invert and injecting cement or cement sodium silicate grout in stages
6 33
GEOLABS HAWAIi
Grouting should be completed at least 45 days prior to tunneling into the
crossover area
To prevent ground loss into the tunnel face the groundwater should
be lowered below invert level along the crossover section using closely
spaced 10 ft or less vacuum wells The wells should be installed after
grouting and should be pumped at least 30 days prior to tunneling Well
discharge can be made behind the bulkhead on the downstream side of the cross
over Dewatering solely from inside the proposed tunnel is not recommended
The proposed tunnel can then be driven beneath the crossover but care
must still be taken to control face movements and ground loss even though the
ground has been grouted Some face breasting should be expected in loose
zones and control of the shield alignment is critical to prevent plowing If
water inflow occurs the face should be protected and allowed to drain and
additional wells should be installed as needed After the initial lining has
been placed in the vicinity of the crossing a thick grout mix should be
injected above the crown of the proposed tunnel through the initial Lining to
fill any loosened zones

The existing sewer at Piper s Pali must be supported prior to tunneling

flows it is recommended that drilled in piles be placed to underpin the

for diverting or conducting flow through the crossing without leakage during

Chevron Gasoline Station
A Chevron gasoline station is located on the northwest corner of Wilder
Avenue and Metcalf Street near Station 50 00 The gasoline station
has four storage tanks located approximately 35 ft from the proposed sewer
line The three original tanks have not been replaced and one tank was added
in 1964
6 35
GEOLABS HAWAII
One of the main concerns of sewer construction near the gas station is
for the possible presence of leaked gasoline in the excavation along this
portion of the alignment The presence of gasoline from leaky storage tanks
has caused serious problens and delays on several underground projects in
metropolitan areas Although no gasoline was detected in the two borings
leaked gasoline may be present in undetected areas because the size of the
exploratory borings is very small compared with the size of the potential
leakage area The contractor should be advised of the possible presence of
gasoline in the excavation and should be prepared to detect and treat flam
mable liquids Treatment and disposal of gasoline contaminated soils if
encountered will be treated as a changed condition
6 7 Blasting
Blasting may be used to excavate rock and boulders encountered along the
alignment in tunnel or braced excavations
Blasting vibrations must be controlled to prevent damage to adjacent
facilities Measurement of the peak particle velocity is used to help control
blasting vibrations Particle velocities in excess of 2 in sec have been
associated with the initiation of cracks in plaster walls
Fall of loose rock in rock tunnels is associated with particle veloci
ties of 12 in sec A well supported tunnel can withstand vibration levels in
excess of 12 in sec The crown of the existing sewer has a tension crack
along much of the section where blasting could be employed The concrete
lining although deteriorated at the inside surface appears coherent and the
crack shows no offset from one side to the other It is recommended that par
ticle velocities at the existing sewer tunnel or at the same distances from
the blast as the existing sewer not exceed 4 in per second for blasts at
distances greater than 50 ft Peak particle velocities in the ground adjacent
to buildings should be less than 2 in sec for blasts at distances greater
than 50 ft For closer blasts the particle velocities can be increased
because the peak vibrations due to close in blasts occur at higher frequencies
and thus do not excite most structures which have lower natural frequencies
6 36
GEOLABS HAWAII
Thus the maximum allowable particle velocities can be increased for distances
less than 50 ft from a structure according to a schedule such as that given
in the contract specifications All delays should be millisecond delays
Precautions should be enployed to minimize air blast noise and prevent
Elyrock Blasting mats should be required in shafts and braced excavations
Blasting should be restricted in residential areas at night
Local and federal safety standards must also be met with respect to
storage and handling An experienced licensed blaster is required to direct
all blasts Blast loadings and vibration levels for each blast should be
documented on a blasting form The contractor should employ a vibration con
sultant to monitor blasts and supply the data to the project manager on the
day that the blasting is carried out
In certain sections of the tunnel it is anticipated that large boulders
or rock layers requiring blasting will be encountered along with soils that
require support Blasting and support should be carried out in such a way
that the ground remains adequately supported in the tunnel face and around the
perimeter during and after the blast
6 8 Existing Sewer Tunnel
The existing sewer is present in the project area beneath Wilder Avenue
between Makiki Street and Piper s Pali At Piper s Pali the line crosses from
the south to the north side of Wilder Avenue and progresses northward along
Piper s Pali The main section of concern for the existing sewer is at and
between the two crossover areas Punahou Street and Piper s Pali where the
proposed line crosses 4 to 6 ft beneath the existing sewer and where the
existing sewer is close enough to be affected by blasting of rock and boulders
in the project excavation The sewer lining is non reinforced concrete with
an inside width of 4 ft and height of 5 ft Information from as built
drawings and inspections of the sewer are included as Appendix K

7 2 Braced Cuts at Crossings of Existing Sewer
Sewer crossing locations are at Station 25 90 to 27 80 where a braced
cut is optional and at Station 41 25 to 41 60 where underpinning anda
braced excavation is required to cross the existing sewer Furthermore the
contractor may elect to extend this excavation to the west of Piper s Pali
Road and use it for a construction shaft
The braced cuts at the sewer crossings mast be constructed in such a way
that movements of the existing sewer are prevented as excavation proceeds
around and below the existing sewer The walls should be supported so that
soil does not run or flow from behind the excavation wall in the vicinity of
the existing sewer Any voids develooed below the existing sewer should be
backfilled
Because of space limitations and the large depth of cuts it will be
necessary to brace the cuts for their full depth so that they stand ver
tically Various combinations of soil rock and boulders will be encountered
in the excavations Soil and rock conditions are shown on the drawings and
are discussed in more detail in Chapters 5 and 6
At the crossing at Station 41 25 to 41 60 the presence of boulders
rock layers and rock masses will make installation of vertical support ele
ments difficult In many cases the rock or boulders that are encountered will
be bounded by soil both above and below Thus it cannot be assumed that
once rock is encountered it will continue to the bottom of the excavation
At both crossing locations it is recommended that vertical support ele
ments be extended to firm material at a minimum depth of 5 feet below the
bottom of the proposed sewer excavation prior to excavating To penetrate
any rock and extend to the full depth it is anticipated that the vertical
support will consist of soldier beams installed in pre drilled holes and back
filled with a lean concrete mix Use of driven sheeting either soldier beams
or sheet piles is not recommended in areas where boulders or rock layers are
anticipated
7 2
GEOLABS HAWAIi
Rock drilling procedures will be needed in areas where large boulders or
basalt layers are present Rock excavation procedures including breakers
and or blasting will also be necessary in these areas to carry out the exca
vation to subgrade between the walls
Figure 7 1 gives apparent earth pressure loadings for the braced cuts
and the procedure for designing the struts
The bending capacity of soldier beams between braces should be designed
using 2 3 of the apparent earth pressure envelope Wales if used should be
designed for full apparent earth pressure
Struts should be installed as the excavation proceeds downward as soon
as possible after excavating to the level of the strut A maximum vertical
strut spacing of 15 ft is recommended Struts should be loaded to a minimum
of 20 of their design capacity at the time of their installation Cross wall
struts are feasible for most of the cuts which are narrow enough to allow
efficient use of the compressive capacity of the strut The upper strut
should be designed for vertical loads if the excavation is to be covered
Lagging between the soldier beams may consist of timber 3 to 4 in thick or
ghoterete reinforced as required to tie it to the soldier beams Lagging
should be installed so that movement and fallout of soil is minimal The
timber lagging should be back packed as needed to ensure firm contact with the
natural soil Where running or flowing of soils tends to occur excavation at
the wall should be limited to distances just sufficient to allow installation
of the next level of lagging
Decking can be placed over the excavation as needed to allow access for
traffic during the period that the excavation is open Deck beams must be
designed for traffic loads as well as lateral earth pressures
In some sections groundwater seepage is expected in and near the base
of the cut as well as perched on top of lower permeability materials above the
7 3
GEOLABS HAWAII
base Water inflows should be controlled to prevent infiltration of fines and
potential instability Dewatering by means of sumos in the excavation and
wells exterior to the excavation should be placed as necessary
Excavation and support of the cut should be carried out in such a way
that damage to utilities and structures is prevented and surface settlements
are minimized Vertical settlement adjacent to well braced excavations in
stiff or dense soils is typically in the range of 2 in or less for cuts in
the range of 30 to 60 ft deep Most of the movement tends to take place
within a lateral distance beyond the top of the wall of 1 5 times the cut
deoth reducing from a maximum near the wall to small amounts away from the
wall Loss of sand or silt into the excavation due to piping could cause
settlements at greater distances
Support and or relocation of utilities that intersect the excavation
will be necessary At the crossing sections support of the existing sewer
will be necessary as described in Chapter 6 Special treatment or relocation
of overhead electrical lines may be needed at and east of Piper s Pali
7 3 Braced Excavation in Wilder Avenue Bast of Piper s Pali
Station 414 25 to 50 00
Sewer construction east of Station 41 25 can be accomplished by
open excavation or tunneling From Station 41 60 to aporoximately Station
45 00 both rock and soil conditions are expected and support conditions are
expected to be similar to those described for the braced excavation at Station
41 25 to 41 60
Further east the deoth of excavation continues to decrease and
although boulders are present in the tuff basalt flows and rock layers will
not be encountered to the degree that they are further west Because of the
snallow depth and the lesser frequency of rock it is expected that excavation
and bracing procedures will not be as difficult The contractor should select
methods that will minimize damage to utilities and structures and disruption
of utilities

8 LOADINGS AND DESIGN FOR TUNNELS EXCAVATION WALL AND SHAFTS
8 1 Final Tunnel Lining
Introduction
In this section the analysis of the loadings on the final lining for
the Increment 2 Honolulu Sewer tunnel system is described The analyses have
been carried out assuming that the final lining is a reinforced concrete pipe
60 in 1 D 6 75 in thick at a depth of 52 ft to the tunnel centerline the
maximum depth in the section
Calculation of Loads
Design loads include the long term loads that will develop on the final
liner The design loads due to external soil and water pressures are as
follows

Structural Behavior of the Final Liner
From the pressure diagrams shown in Figure 8 1 the major load com
ponents on the final liner can be determined These load components are the
Thrust T and the Maximum Moment M acting on the liner The calculated
Thrust T and Moment M in the lining are multiplied by a load factor and
are then compared with the ultimate capacity of the lining by plotting them on
a moment thrust interaction diagram If the plotted point falls within the
envelope then the lining has enough capacity to withstand the soil loads
Thrust and Moment Calculations
The Thrust and Moment that develop in the lining under the pressures
shown in Figure 8 1 depend on the degree of soil structure interaction A
8 2
GEOLABS HAWAII
liner is said to be flexible if it interacts with the surrounding soil in
such a way that the lining is able to deflect until the pressure distribution
around the lining is equalized resulting in negligible bending moments at all
points in the lining A rigid liner is one which deflects insignificantly
under the loads imposed by the soil thus there is almost no soil structure
interaction and the pressures around the lining do not equalize Asa
result moments remain high The degree of soil structure interaction can be
evaluated in terms of the relative stiffness of the lining and the surrounding
soil This relative stiffness can be quantified in terms of the flexibility
ratio which is defined as

V_ poisson s Modulus of concrete
R Average radius of the liner
Linings will behave essentially as flexible liners if the flexibility ratio is
larger than 10

Discussion of Conservatism of the Analysis
The vertical loads used in the calculations shown above correspond to
full overburden pressure above the tunnel In most cases the loads that
develop around a tunnel are equivalent to the weight of soil immediately
around the opening which is proportional to the width and height of the tun
nel rather than to the total depth of overburden A more realistic estimate
of the pressures acting on the final liner can be obtained from Ch 7 Proctor
and white 1977 recommendations regarding load on tunnel support on running
8 6
GEOLABS HAWAII
ground These recommendations indicate that the load on the roof of a tunnel
support in running ground doesn t exceed a small fraction of the weight of the
ground above the roof unless the cover is very shallow If the depth of the
overburden is greater than about 1 5 times the combined width and height of
the tunnel the load is practically independent of depth Available data
indicates that under these circumstances in a tunnel excavated in loose
sands the roof support will carry a maximum load equivalent to the weight of
a soil column with a height of Hp which can be estimated as
width of the tunnel and

8 2 Initial Tunnel Lining
Soil Loadin
The effect of soil loading on the initial tunnel lining installed by
the contractor can be evaluated using procedures similar to those given in
Section 8 1 However if the initial lining is much more flexible than the
final lining as is expected to be the case interaction will take place bet
ween the lining and the soil as the lining deflects building up passive reac
tions resulting in maximum moments in the lining that are less than those that
would develop in a rigid lining subjected to the same initial loading
Therefore the maximum moments will be less than obtained from the following
relation for a rigid lining
Analyses of circular linings in an elastic medium subjected to an ini
tial vertical pressure ov and horizontal pressure o Kov provide a means
of determining the maximum moment as a function of the flexibility ratio
Peck Ranken R E et al 1978 Peck 1969 recom
diametral deflection AD D of 0 5 Assuming an elliptically shaped defor
mation pattern the maximum moment would be
m
R D R
For the initial lining the moments that develop will be strongly
influenced by the method of installation and the uniformity of the backpacking
or contact between the lining and the surrounding ground For example large
overbreak that is incompletely filled will produce large local moments in the
lining It is recommended that the initial lining be designed to accommodate
a minimum thrust T due to the weight of the height of soil equal to at least
3 times the excavated tunnel diameter with a minimum moment of 0 03TR or the
moment obtained by considering the flexibility relationship between soil and
lining whichever is larger
8 9
GEOLABS HAWAII
Other Loads
One of the primary loadings for which the initial lining must be
designed is the thrust of the tunnel shield The lining must be designed to
accommodate localized thrust from jacks applied at one side of the shield
only
8 3 Shaft Design
General
Shaft linings may be installed against the excavated surface of the soil
may be backfilled around a shaft lining For soil backfilled around the
shaft at rest pressures equal to KyH should be assumed where K 0 5
For shaft linings placed against the excavated soil with increasing
depth the lateral earth pressures will reach a constant value which is propor
tional to the shaft diameter Proctor and white 1977 give values for
pressures on walls of shafts excavated in running ground
The design pressure acting on the shaft walls at a depth o
aft wa

Recommendations for Manholes and Structures
with Soil Backfilled Around Their Perimeter
All linings in which soil is backfilled around the perimeter should be
designed for at rest pressures For all circular manholes regardless of the
method of installation a nominal lining will be adequate for all possible
loadings It is therefore recommended that the more conservative at rest
pressures be used in their design as shown in Figure 8 3
Recommendations for Construction Shaft Linings
Shaft linings installed against the excavated surface during construc
tion should be designed for the apparent earth pressures used for braced exca
vations Section 8 4 However it may be possible to reduce apparent earth
pressures for initial linings in the smaller diameter shafts by substituting
a value equal to 2 times the shaft width for the shaft depth in the deter
mination of the magnitude of the apparent earth pressure envelope
8 4 Braced Excavations
Initial support for braced excavations should be designed using the
apparent earth pressure envelope given in Figure 7 1 The pressure diagram
assumes that the excavation is braced from the top down as excavation proceeds
downward Thus the braces in the upper level of the cut will restrain the
cutt walls and receive a significant load
Criteria for spacing of braces and excavation procedures are outlined in
Chapter 7

APPENDIX A
DRILLING EQUIPMENT
AND SAMPLING DETAILS
APPENDIX A
Equipment and Sampling Detaiis
Subsurface explorations Phases I and II were conducted by the
drilling of forty one 41 borings ranging in depth from 29 4 to 73 feet
below the existing ground surface for the proposed sewer tunnel
Increment 2 The borings were drilled by a total of two 2 drill
rigs a CME 55 and CME 45 both are truck mounted
Most of the borings were advanced by continuous sampling using ring
sampler and split spoon Four inch 4 diameter solid stem flight
augers and 7 5 inch hollow stem augers were used to clean out the
borings after each split spoon sampling intervals and prior to using the
ring sampler In cases where two consecutive standard penetration tests
were carried out the depths between sampling intervals were carefully
checked to ensure double sampling did not take place
Rotary wash borings using 4 inch casings were required in Boring
In order to study the effect of different sampling techniques on
the conditions of samples recovered the Triefus HMLC triple tube core
barrel was used to obtain core samples at selected locations Boring
Nos G 4 and G 13 The Triefus HMLC barrel was also used to obtain
core samples of rock boulder corals encountered in Boring Nos G 16
17b 18 and 20
Fresh water was the main drilling fluids used Drill mud was used
in Boring No G l the first boring completed to ensure good return of
cuttings Revert drill muc was used in Boring No G 4 from 49 foot
depth to advance casing because the loose cinder sand has gripped the
4 inch casing and has earlier caused damage to the drive chain of the
CME 45
Boring 16 17 and 19 were successfully drilled using
4 inch diameter solid stem angers
During drilling of Boring Nos G 5 14 and 21 the loose or soft
cinder sands or silts appeared to grip the hollow stem augers at depths
below 40 feet In Boring No G 21 the saturated loose cinder sand
actually flowed 5 feet into the hollow stem upon retrieval of the inner
rods This problem was overcome by filling the hollow stem with water
prior to pulling of the inner rods and the head developed inside the
hollow stem has successfully prevented the cinder sand from flowing in
GEOLABS HAWAI
Appendix A cont d
I D 1880 2
Soil samples were obtained from the borings by the use of drive
samplers and the Triefus triple tube core barrels The drive samples
were taken by driving a standard penetration sampler 2 inch 0 D split
hammer falling 30 inches The blow counts to drive the sampler for each
6 inch penetration are shown on the log of the borings at the
appropriate sample depths
The Triefus HMLC core barrel was able to cut through all materials
encountered on site and obtain good quality sample However in
situations where soft silt or clay underly cinder sand or boulders the
much weaker silty clay was not able to push the denser or harder cores
into the barrel and have resulted in core lost in such cases
A Dennison barrel was used to obtain core sample in cinder sand in
Boring No G4 Its performance in terms of core recovery was not as
good as those of the Triefus triple tube barrel and mechanical
breakdown has terminated the use of the barrel
Both NX double tube and Wireline core barrels HQ were
successfully used to obtain rock core samples in basalt lava flows in

APPENDIX C
FIELD PERMEABILITY TESTS
APPENDIX C
Permeability Testing
Thirty two 32 falling head permeability tests were performed both
in the drilled holes and inside the installed piezometer during the
course of our field exploration
Field permeabilities were calculated based on methods suggested by
Hvorslev 1951 Time Log and Soil Permeability in Ground Water
Observation Bulletin No 36 Waterways Experiment Station Corps of
Engineer Vicksburg MISS
For falling head tests carried out inside the drilled holes the
tested sections were the uncased portion of the holes between the
bottom of the casing and a bottom bentonite seal or generally in porous
horizons between less permeable silt or clay layers In the latter
case the bottom of the test section was plugged by a bentonite seal
For falling head tests carried out inside the installed
piezometers the tested sections were usually isolated by top and bottom
bentonite seals
The falling head test data are summarized on Plates C 1 through
C 54
Pump tests were originally planned to be performed at the two
observation shaft locations Approximate Station 16 00 and 33 00
However it was only carried out at Station 33 00 Wilder Avenue
because of variation in the elevation of the perching unit and the
shallow depth 1 foot of the perched water table at the Makiki District
Park Station 16 00 location
Each test site consisted of a pumping well with an array of
observation wells placed at varying depths distances and directions
from the pump well
To install a pump well a 12 inch diameter auger was used to
over drill 3 to 5 feet the bottom of both 42 inch diameter observation
shafts A 5 foot long 6 inch O D slotted PVC pipe with fiberglass
screen wrapping was placed in each over drilled hole Cuttings inside
the 6 inch PVC pipes were removed by means of water flushing and
air lifting with an air compressor
Initially a Berkeley Model 5 H P single phase
submersible pump was used for the pump test Hand bailing was
subsequently adopted because the shallow depth of the perched water
table did not permit the electrical pump to be used throughout the test
Locations of the test sites are shown in Drawing and 5
and Plate C 59 Details of the construction and geometry of the wells
and the resulting data are presented in Plates C 55 to C 59

APPENDIX D
PIEZOMETER DETAILS
AND GROUND WATER LEVEL
MONITORING RECORDS
APPENDIX D
Piezometer Details and Groundwater Level Monitoring
A total of thirty one 31 single level and four 4 multi level
piezometers were installed in different boreholes to allow long term
monitoring of groundwater levels
The plezometer generally consists of a three fourths of an inch PVC
riser pipe with a bottom slotted section enclosed by fiberglass screen
Backfill around the slotted section consists of either silica sand No
20 black sand or black sand and gravel Above and generally below the
slotted section bentonite seals were applied
A graphical presentation of the piezometer details which includes
the depths and different types of backfill materials is shown on
Piezometer Details Plates D 1 through D 35
Periodic readings of the piezometric levels were made A summary
of the readings taken in 1985 which consists only of those of
Piezometer No G l is presented on Plate D 36 The readings taken for
all the piezometers since the beginning of 1986 are summarized on Plates
D 3 7 and D 38
W O 1618 00 MAY 1986

APPENDIX D
PIEZOMETER DETAILS
AND GROUND WATER LEVEL
MONITORING RECORDS
APPENDIX D
Piezometer Details and Groundwater Level Monitoring
A total of thirty one 31 single level and four 4 multi level
piezometers were installed in different boreholes to allow long term
monitoring of groundwater levels
The plezometer generally consists of a three fourths of an inch PVC
riser pipe with a bottom slotted section enclosed by fiberglass screen
Backfill around the slotted section consists of either silica sand No
20 black sand or black sand and gravel Above and generally below the
slotted section bentonite seals were applied
A graphical presentation of the piezometer details which includes
the depths and different types of backfill materials is shown on
Piezometer Details Plates D 1 through D 35
Periodic readings of the piezometric levels were made A summary
of the readings taken in 1985 which consists only of those of
Piezometer No G l is presented on Plate D 36 The readings taken for
all the piezometers since the beginning of 1986 are summarized on Plates
D 3 7 and D 38
W O 1618 00 MAY 1986

PLATE D 38
GEOLABS HAWAILT
APPENDIX E
LABORATORY TESTING
APPENDIX
Laboratory Testing
email
The moisture content unit weight and Atterberqg Limit
determinations were performed on selected samples as an aid in the
classification and evaluation of soil properties The results of the
moisture and Atterberg Limit tests are presented on the Logs of Borings
Appendix B at the appropriate sample depths The Atterberg Limits are
further summarized on Plates E 1 through E 33
Seventy nine 79 sieve analyses were performed on samples of
cinder sand to evaluate its grain size distribution The numerical
results of the tests are summarized on Plates E l through E 32 A
graphical presentation of all the results are shown on Plates E 34
through E 5l
Eleven inch diameter ring swell tests were performed on
natural samples of the silty clays and clayey silt to evaluate the
swelling potential of the soils The test results are summarized on
Plate E 52
Four 4 unconfined compression tests were performed on core
samples of the cemented cinder sand and silty clays to evaluate
respective uniaxial compressive strength and undrained strength The
test results are presented on Plate F 53
Ten 10 sets of total number 30 direct shear tests were carried
out on both cemented and loose cinder sand silty clay and clayey silt
to determine the effective shear strength parameters Separate
undisturbed sample was used for each individual load increment The
results of the tests on uncemented cinder sand are summarized on Plate
E 54 The summary for slightly cemented sand is shown on Plate E 55
Results of tests on marine clay alluvium and weathered tuff are shown
on Plates E 56 through E 61
Based on the results of the unconfined compression tests and the
direct shear tests a summary of recommendation on soil parameters for
earth pressure calculation was prepared The summary table which
At re lnd
contains the recommended friction angle cohesion and total unit weight
for various types of material encountered in this project is shown on
Plate E 62
One 1 moisture density determination was performed on a bulk
sample of cinder sand to determine the compaction characteristics of the
cinder material The sample was collected from cuttings stockpiled
during excavation of the large diameter observation shaft at Makiki
District Park The test results are plotted on Plate E 70
GEOLABS HAWAIi
Appendix E cont d 2
Two 2 consolidation tests were performed oon undisturbed
fine grained samples to evaluate the compressibility and settlement
characteristics The test results are presented on Plates E 63 and E 64
Two 2 swell pressure tests were performed on undisturbed and
reconsolidated soil samples from Boring Nos G 12 and G 15 The purpose
of the test was to evaluate the potential swell pressure of the clayey
silt and silty clay type of soil under the conditions of stress relief
during the tunnel excavation The results of the tests are presented on
Plate E 65
In conjunction with the laboratory tests performed in Honolulu by
Geolabs Hawaii a series of laboratory tests on rock samples were
carried out in the rock mechanics laboratory Department of Civil
Engineering at the University of Illinois at Urbana Champaign The
purpose of the tests was to determine the unconfined compressive
strength and the hardness of the rock samples A summary of the
unconfined compression test results is shown on Plate E 66 Summaries
of the Abrasion Hardness test the Schmidt L Hammer test and the
Scleroscope Hardness tests are shown on Plates E 67 E 68 and E 69
respectively

APPENDIX F
ELECTRIC CONE PENETRATION
TESTING
APPENDIX F
Electric Cone Penetration Tests
Two electric cone penetration tests were performed in cinder
The cone penetrometer test consists of hydraulically pushing a
steel cone with an apex angle of 60 degrees and an area of 10 square
centimeters connected to a friction sleeve with an area of 150 square
centimeters into the ground The tip and side friction resistance
induced during penetration are measured by strain gages within the
instrument and are recorded versus the depth of penetration as shown on
Plates F 1 through F 3
The values of the tip resistance and friction ratio sleeve
friction over tip resistance can be correlated to soil strength and
classification For sandy soil both the peak friction angle and the
relative density are important soil parameters These parameters were
interpretated with the use of graphs developed by Baldi et al 1982
and Robertson and Campanella 1983 The results of the interpretation
are shown on Plates F 2 and F 4

APPENDIX G
SEISMIC GEOLOGY
AND SEISMICITY
APPENDIX G
Seismic Geology and Seismicity
mt eh me ny
There has been thirteen 13 major earthquakes with magnitudes
ranging from 4 7 to 7 2 between 1929 and 1981 within the State of
Hawaii Cox 1985 The Oahu intensities of these earthquakes which
have known magnitudes and epicentral distances are shown on Table l
In the State of Hawaii the strongest earthquake in historic times
occurred on and was epicentered along the south coast of
the Island of Hawaii This earthquake had a Richter magnitude of about
7 5 and caused severe damage across the entire island On Oahu some
pendulum clocks were stopped Furumoto et al 1973
The seismic zone map for Hawaii Uniform Building Code which was
compiled by The U S Coast and Geodetic Survey in 1949 Furumoto et al
1973 assigned a Zone 1 seismic risk rating for Oahu Zone 1 is
defined as Minor damage corresponding to an intensity on the Modified
Mercalli Scale of less than 6 This is being used as the legal basis
for establishing earthquake design criteria Furumoto et al 1973
Furumoto et al 1973 postulated that this seismic zoning was based
solely on the earthquake damage which is the largest ever
recorded in the State of Hawaii However the epicenter of this event
was located close to the island of Hawaii
The highest estimated average intensities experienced by Oahu was
rated VI to VII MM scale as a result of an earthquake that occurred on
epicentered between the islands of Molokai and Maui
The Richter magnitude was estimated to be 7 or greater Furumoto et al
1973 reasoned that the seismic risk for Oahu should be determined from
damaging earthquakes that have occurred close to the East Molokai
Fracture Zone and not from earthquakes epicentered close to the island
of Hawaii
Based on factual information Furumoto et al 1973 proposed a
change in the seismic zoning for the Hawaiian Islands and a Zone 2
seismic rating was proposed for Oahu Zone 2 is defined as Moderate
damage corresponding to an intensity on the Modified Mercalli Scale of
less than 7 In recent years the Departments of Army Navy and Air
Force jointly assigned Zone 2 seismic rating for Oahu NAVFAC P B55
Many structural engineers in Honolulu routinely use the lateral force
requirements specified for Zone 2 Furumoto et al 1973

From data 19 events analysed by the method of the statistics of
extremes Furumoto 1981 concluded that Honolulu can expect an
intensity VI event every 30 years an intensity VII every 100 years and
an intensity VIII every 280 years In terms of exceedence Honolulu can
expect an event to exceed intensity VI every 50 years to exceed
intensity VII every 150 years and to exceed intensity VIII every 700
years
Based on available data the faults and fissures in Hawaii are
shown on Figure 1 with selected epicenters Faults in the eastern side
of the region were identified by bathymetric data Magnetic lineations
are shown as extension of the faults identified by aeromagnetic surveys
Malahoft and Woollard 1965
In correlating these features with earthquake epicenters Furumoto
1981 identified Figure 2
a the Diamond Head Fault a linear arrangement of earthquakes
going through Diamond Head Koko Crater and trending in a
east northeast direction One earthquake of estimated
magnitude 5 1948 and two of recorded magnitudes 4 1977 were
located along this fault Estill 1979
b the Molokai Seismic Zone a belt of earthquakes going through
Molokai among which 2 earthquakes of major proportion 1871
and 1938 occurred in this belt magnitude 7 The west end
of the belt is less than 31 miles from Honolulu
Furumoto 1981 postulated that the worst credible earthquake on
the Diamond Head fault would have a magnitude of 6 just offshore from
Diamond Head On the Molokai Seismic Zone the worst credible
earthquake would occur under the Island of Molokai with a magnitude of
7 5 or the intensity felt at Honolulu would be VII

Appendix G cont d C 6
Experience indicates that underground structures in general and
tunnels in particular are relatively insensitive to damage from
earthquake effects unless an actual fault plane crosses the tunnel and
shear imovements on the fault damage the tunnel lining It is understood
that this situation does not apply to the sewer alignment presently
being designed hence this subject is not considered a major controlling
criteria in tunnel design
A possible mode of earthquake damage is lining distortion and
stress imposed by earthquake induced seismic waves travelling through
the soil around the tunnel The structural behavior of the tunnel
lining under earthquake loading can be evaluated by determining the
distortions imposed upon the lining by the dynamic strains in the
surrounding soil mass induced by the earthquake waves
The magnitude of the earthquake induced strains in the liner can
be approximated as follow Merrit et al 1985

be easily accommodated by cast in place or precast concrete segments

APPENDIX K
EXISTING SEWER TUNNEL
APPENDIX Kk
Existing Sewer Tunnel
K 1 Construction History and Character of Existing Tunnel
The existing sewer in the project area was constructed as part of the
first section of the Manoa Kaimuki Interceptor The 18839 ft long interceptor
between Kinau Street and Palolo Avenue was built as a tunnel in 1928 and 1929
The only construction data available in the files of the Division of Wastewater
Management were a set of as built plans These plans are available for
examination at the Geotechnical Consultant s office
A 1929 report published by the Honolulu Sewer and Water Commission was
found in the library and contains a brief description of the project The bid
price was 700 000 but overruns of more than 40 occurred as a result of dif
ficult ground The following paragraphs are taken directly from the 1929
report
During July 1928 a contract was let to BE J Lord Ltd
for the first section of the Manoa Kaimuki Interceptor obli
gating the Commission for the sum of which was the
extent of the funds available Three bids were received on this
project and while the bids were also over the estimate the
usual uncertainties of tunnel work were present and it was
thought best to let this project as the bids were received from
the largest contractors in the Territory
In connection with this project funds should be made
available to complete it the estimated amount needed being
Owing to bad ground a great deal more timbering
has been required than was estimated from the borings
The as built plans show a 4 ft wide by 5 ft high unreinforced concrete
lined tunnel Type C section throughout the project area Fig K 1 The
plans show a lining thickness of 6 in of which aporoximately 1 in appears to
be vitrified clay blocks tile The lining contains an unreinforced shear
key or joint between the invert and the walls The plans show no hydraulic
K 1
GEOLABS HAWAII
barrier in the shear key and water leakage along the joint is inevitable
Initial support was provided by timber which consisted of 4 by 4 in timber
sets and 2 by 12 in wood lagging The character and extent of backpacking
behind the timber support is unknown
The as built construction details show that the existing sewer will be
very sensitive to undermining in the crossover areas The lack of reinfor
cement particularly along the shear key makes the existing sewer very
vulnerable to loss of ground during excavation and support of the proposed
tunnel Ground losses could easily cause opening and possible collapse of the
invert Any leakage from the existing line would reduce ground stability and
accelerate ground loss problems Special measures will be required to prevent
flooding of the proposed tunnel and loss of sewer service
K Ahn Associates Study 1971
In late 1969 the Division of Wastewater Management authorized an inspection
of most of the Manoa Kaimuki Interceptor including the section in the project area
At least four reports were prepared by the consulting group headed by Chung Dho
Ahn Associates in collaboration with A A Mathews Inc and James M Montgomery
Inc Sections of the A A Mathews reports 1971 pertinent to the project are
quoted in the following paragraphs
TT Observations
D Metcalf Street to Punahor
was habe Ri Ne Neth ett At Ne Sacto fd
Keeaumoku Street
Significant cracks in the arch of the tunnel concrete lining and
voids in the ground above the lining were observed throughout the
portion of tunnel inspected between Alexander Street and Keeaumoku
Street Narrow cracks were present between Alexander and Halekula
Streets Between Halekula and Punahou Streets open cracks as much
as 6 inches wide were observed extending through the full depth of
K 2
GEOLABS HAWAII
the concrete lining Voids behind the lining 3 to 12 inches deep
and extending the full width of the tunnel were observed through
these cracks Approximately 100 feet upstream from Punahou Street a
piece of concrete approximately 6 inches wide by 8 inches deep by 6
feet long was hanging with one end wedged in the crack and one end
resting on the invert Maintenance crews report that this section of
the tunnel was last inspected aporoximately 5 years ago and no cracks
were visible at that time Apparently most tile were still in place
then
The crack generally is located within approximately 10 inches
from the center of the crown but occasionally is as much as 20
inches down the arch from the center Voids behind the crack were
not probed for the full length of tunnel inspected between Alexander
and Keeaumoku Streets so it is not known if the voids described above
represent the largest voids which exist above the lining
A profile of the tunnel supplied by Division of Sewers in
January 1970 indicates cracks along Wilder Avenue between Keeaumoku
and Kewalo Streets were observed in 1961 It is not known whether
patching of these cracks by sewer maintenance crews has remained in
place to the present nor if cracking now extends downstream beyond
Kewalo Street The length of tunnel which is known to have contained
these cracks extends along Wilder Avenue from near Alexander Street
approximately downstream to near Kewalo Street
approximately for a total of approximately 3050 feet
Ooservation in the tunnel both in the areas of most severe
cracking along Wilder Avenue and in other areas indicates much if
not all of the concrete lining of the tunnel built in 1923 was
constructed with longitudinal cold joints in the arch generally
within 10 inches of the center of the crown As built drawings of
the tunnel indicate this portion of tunnel along Wilder Avenue was
constructed with timber primary supports It is hypothesized that
rotting of these timber supports has reduced the lateral support of
K 3
GEOLABS HAWAII
the tunnel lining and allowed internal water pressure to open the
lining along the longitudinal cold joints It is known that movement
has actually occurred because pictures from the files of the Division
of Sewers believed to have been taken in the early 1960 s when the
eracks were first discovered show cracks of 1 to 2 inches width in
the clay tile spanning the cracks in the concrete lining It is
believed that the pieces of concrete up to 6 inches wide and 6 feet
long which have fallen from the crown may represent narrow fillings
between cold joints in the original lining and that opening of the
lining along these cold joints has loosened these fillings and
allowed them to fall
Inspection of adjacent portions of the tunnel along Wilder Avenue
between Alexander Street approximately and Keeaumoku
Street on 1970 revealed more exten
sive cracking of the concrete in the tunnel crown and larger voids
above the crown than had been observed in the inspection between
Punahou Street and Makiki Street described in the
report In spaces a ruler could be extended 5 feet above the
concrete lining Voids as much as 4 feet wide extending 8 to 12
inches above the concrete were observed Probing with a folding
ruler indicated the existence in places of voids 4 feet wide
extending 2 feet or more in height above the concrete lining
Lining damage and voids above the arch of the existing sewer between
Makiki and Punahou Streets are summarized in Fig K 2 The profile was taken
from the as built construction drawings for repair of this section of the
sewer in 1973
K 3 Repair Work
One of the recommendations of the study was to repair the
most extensively cracked and damaged sections of the sewer line Basically
the repairs involved plywood forming of the tunnel surface and then injecting
grout and light weight concrete in the borenoles drilled from the ground
K 4
GEOLABS HAWAII
surface to patch the lining and fill voids behind the lining Repair oroce
dures are illustrated in Fig K 2 The repair work was carried out in 1973
shortly after completion of the report
The plans for the repair work indicate that no repairs were made east of
Punahou Street The Wastewater Management records seem to verify the as built
drawings however based on our tunnel inspection some repairs have been made
for a distance of 150 ft east of Punahou Street
K 4 Sewer Inspection for This Project
K 4 1 General
Between and 1 30 am on 5 and 6 May 1986 members of the design
team inspected the existing sewer between Piper s Pali and Punahou Streets
At the time of the visit the wastewater depth was aporoximately 26 to 30 in
above the tunnel invert Flow velocity ranged between 1 8 to sec and
the estimated discharge was 5 mgd Based on the sharp con
tinuous line between tiled and spalled tile portions of the tunnel lining
approximately 2 ft above invert the wastewater level and thus the minimum
discharge appears to be relatively uniform Higher flows 40 in above tun
nel invert are recorded in the A A Mathews reports and full flow under
pressure is possible during periods of heavy rain No visual observations
could be made below the wastewater level At the time of our inspection the
valve controlling the 24 in diameter diversion line to the East End Interceptor
was fully open and the line appeared to be flowing about 25 full
The general conditions in the 57 year old sewer are illustrated in
Fig K 3 Above the wastewater line nearly all of the tiles have fallen off
the surface of the lining Moreover the tiles were present in only 3 short
sections between Piper s Pali and Punahou Street Most of the tiles appear to
be present just below wastewater level out many appear to bé partially rotated
out away from the wall
K 5
GEOLABS HAWAIi
The floor of the existing tunnel was covered with debris including
tiles and sediment The depth of the debris varied between 2 to 12 in with
an average depth of 6 in
The surface of the tunnel lining above the wastewater line is grooved
and contains remnants of the edges of the tiles that have fallen from the
lining
In general the inner 3 4 to 1 in of the tunnel lining behind the
fallen tile was soft and altered and could be scraped away with the claw end
of a hammer In some local sections the concrete was soft to a depth of
to 2 in Hard concrete with unweathered aggregate is present behind the soft
layer and the contact between hard and soft concrete is relatively sharp
Thus a hard concrete lining in the arch may be as little as 4 in thick if
no deterioration has occurred on the outside face of the lining
K 4 2 Piper s Pali Crossing
The existing sewer at the Piper s Pali Crossing appeared to be uncracked
and structurally sound except for a 5 ft long section at existing sewer
Station 173 00 Fig K 3 which has a narrow longitudinal crack on the east
side of the crown The crack is located in one of the three sections that
contain the original tile in the arch 58 ft northwest of the sewer manhole at
Piper s Pali and Wilder Avenue The crack appears to tensile and no offset
was seen on opposite sides of the crack The crack was wet apparently from
seepage of groundwater through the lining
K 4 3 Section Between Crossings
The existing sewer between Piper s Pali and Alexander Street Stations
172 41 and 168 10 shows no evidence of cracking or wet areas in the crown
west of the sewer manhole at Alexander Street Station
167 60 the lining is cracked in the arch intermittently to within 150 ft east
of the existing sewer manhole at Punahou Street This includes the section of
proposed tunnel in the Punahou crossover area Dre 4 The crack is
K 6
GEOLABS HAWAII
typically 1 8 to 1 2 in wide and located 2 to 10 in left view downstream
of the crown Fig K 3 The wider cracks appear to be related to fallout of
concrete in the crown The crack appears to be continuous through the lining
and in a few places a saw blade penetrated up to 1 ft behind the tunnel sur
face Thus some voids are present above the crown of the opening The crown
crack appears to have been formed in tension and was most likely produced by
internal wastewater pressure acting inside the tunnel lining Moreover there
is no measureable offset between opposite sides of the cracks that indicate
shear displacements and or uneven loading of the lining Based on a few
hammer probes the lining does not have drumny areas near the Piper s Pali
manhole
Longitudinal construction joints were observed at two locations
Stations 159 54 and 166 64 The construction joints sloped approximately
15 from horizontal and were open approximately 1 8 in
Wet spots were observed in four short sections between the crossings
Stations 163 15 and 164 34 The wet spots are typically 2
to 3 ft long and appear to have seepage of fresh water because there was no
appreciable corrosion of concrete in the wet area Three of the four wet
spots were associated with the crack in the left side of the crown The other
wet area was located on the right north side of tunnel approximately 1 ft
below the crown Station 161 54 Iron staining occurred in the wet spot at
Station 163 15
K Street Crossing
The Punahou Street crossing is located at aporoximate Station 158 50 in
the existing sewer Fig K 3 The crossover is positioned in the repaired
section of the sewer east of Punahou Street The lining at the present
crossover point is located in an area where the repaired crown cracks are
0 2 to 0 5 feet wide on the left side of the crown
K 7
GEOLABS HAWAIl
Between Stations 157 77 to 159 27 Longitudinal cracks in the
lining appear to have been repaired even though there are no records of
repairs east of Punahou Street in the Wastewater Management files This
section is located in the crossover area at Punahou Street and was the
most seriously damaged section identified in the study
The repair appears to be a mortar filling of cracks which provides
structurally continuity to the arch The mortar has good bond to the
adjacent concrete and no sections of mortar have fallen out of the arch
or walls The mortar filled cracks are typically 2 in wide and are
generally located on the left south side of the arch In some places
the filling is up to 1 ft wide The mortar repair has corroded to a
depth of 1 2 to 1 in similar to the concrete Hard mortar is present
behind the soft corroded zone The method of mortar placement and the
extent of voids above the tunnel lining are not known

APPENDIX L
LARGE DIAMETER 42 INCH
OBSERVATION SHAFTS
APPENDIX L
Large Diameter 42 Inch Observation Shafts
Ll Introduction
During the period May 27 to two approximately
42 in diameter calyx holes were drilled and cased near the proposed align
ment The first hole CH 1 was excavated primarily in cinder sand just north
of the proposed construction shaft in Makiki Park Drawing 2 The second
hole CH 2 was drilled within 15 ft of Boring G 14 along Wilder Avenue
approximately 320 ft west of Alexander Street Drawing 4 The locations were
selected to expose conditions in two of the main geological sections along the
proposed alignment cinder sand and cinder silt interbeds in Reach 2 and
mixed soil and rock conditions in Reaches 3 and 4
Both observation shafts were cased after drilling with 42 in ID
1 4 in thick steel pipe placed in one piece to the bottom of the hole Two
one foot square windows were cut every 2 ft on opposite sides of the pipe
Every other pair of windows is rotated 180
The results of the observation shafts work is summarized in the
following paragraphs
L 2 Observation Shafts CH l Makiki Park
L 2 1 Equipment and Drilling Operations
Observation Shaft CH l was drilled using a 42 in diameter drilling
bucket mounted on a 2 stage telescoping Kelly which was driven by 140 H P
International Motor The drilling rig was a truck mounted Earth Drill Model
150 The Kelly was 24 ft long and the bucket capable of drilling
L 1
GEOLABS HAWAIiI
in one run Rig down pressure which was principally provided by the
weight of the bucket and the Kelly was approximately 2 tons The bucket was
equipped with carbide tipped cutting teeth
The hole was drilled to a depth of 56 ft in roughly 8 hours total
working time The average rate of excavation was hour
during the time the drill was operating In the upper 20 to 40 ft of the hole
drilling rates exceeded 17 ft hour min over 10 ft increments during
operation of the rig Plate L1 1 and L1 2
Drilling of the calyx hole in the cinder sand and interbedded cinders
and silt was easy the bucket rig had no difficulty drilling through the
materials In addition the sides of the large diameter hole were very stable
except in the cinders below the water table at a depth of 53 5 to
Moreover the hole was left uncased approximatly 24 hours after comoletion
with no noticeable casing or loss of material along the sides of the hole
Some minor piping of cinders occurred in the bottom of the hole during and
shortly after drilling when ground water was flowing into the hole Once the
ground water level equilibrated there was essentially no loss of material into
the excavated hole
The only difficulty in excavating observation shaft CH 1 was running of
the excavated cinders out of the slots in the bucket below a depth of 5 ft
In many runs particularly in the clean cinders almost all of the excavated
material fell back in the hole before the bucket reached the ground surface
At a depth of 18 ft steel plate flaps were placed on the bucket to reduce
loss of excavated cinders when the bucket was pulled from the hole In addi
tion water was added to the cuttings during drilling to provide some apparent
cohesion to the disturbed cinders The plates and the water helped cut loss
of excavated material by roughly 50 to 70 however some material was lost
the bucket and the flaps
L 2
GEOLABS HAWAII
L 2 2 Casing Installation
On May 30th two days after the start of drilling a 54 ft long 42 in
ID casing was installed in the 45 in diameter excavated hole The upper 30
ft of hole was drilled 4 in out of plumb ft and the casing was sup
ported by the soil at a depth of 34 ft From 34 to the casing was
advanced by raising and then free falling the pipe In the next 5 ft the
casing was pushed by a different drill rig Watson 3000 and was finally
driven the last foot by the Kelly bar The lower portion of the casing
was filled with of debris
L 2 3 Soil Conditions and Ground Behavior
The soil conditions encountered in the observation shaft were logged
during and after drilling and are summarized below Additional subsurface
data can be obtained from the log of Boring G 5 which is located approximately
25 ft from CH l see Appendix B
The log of CH 1 shows brown silty sand from 0 to with a layer of
cobbles between a deoth of 8 to 9 ft From 12 5 to tunnel crown
the shaft was excavated in dark brown to black medium to coarse cinder sand
CINDER with layers and lenses of Light brown silt and sandy silt The silt
layers are typically 0 2 to thick and are generally spaced ft
apart The spacing between the silt layers tends to increase below a depth of
31 ft Based on the results of Boring G 5 the blow counts in the cinders
SP SM above the tunnel crown averages 31 blows ft range 13 to 53
blows ft The cinder unit contains some cobble size blocks of welded cinder
None of the cobble sized materials in either the cinders or overlying silty
sand caused problems in excavating the caisson hole
In the tunnel interval the cinder sand is black medium to coarse grained
and weakly cemented N values in the tunnel interval average 28 blows ft and
range between 23 and 35 blows ft see Appendix B ft thick silt
layer dipping away from the mountains was encountered in the arch of the tun
nel A layer of brown clayey silt thick was encountered 2 ft below the
L 3
GEOLABS HAWAII
invert of the proposed tunnel The cinder silt contact was present at a
higher elevation than the same contact at Boring G 5 The ground water
perched on the silt in the calyx hole is at the invert of the proposed tunnel
Study of the materials exposed in the casing windows shows that the cinder
sand from 12 5 to is weakly cemented fused and or bonded with
silt There was no evidence of running in the cinders or silt Moreover the
soils above the water table in the vertical shaft walls are stable Below the
water table the cinders exhibited flowing behavior when there was a gradient
toward the calyx hole Overall the standup time in cemented cinders should
be good if the tunnel is 2 ft or more above the ground water table
Although the interbedded cinders and silt between 26 and are
stable in CH l similarly described units particularly in Reach 1 are
not expected to be as stable in the tunnel excavation because the cinders
are reworked not cemented and the silt layers are more frequent In
general the N values are lower in the interbeds of Reach 1 as compared
with Reach 2 Moreover some running and ravelling ground is expected in
the tunnel excavation in Reach 1 In addition more extensive flowing ground
problems will occur if the tunnel in Reach 1 is at or below the water table
than occurred in the bottom of observation shaft CH 1
3 Observation Shaft CH 2 Mixed Rock and Soil Section
L 3 1 Equipment and Drilling Operations
The second calyx hole was drilled over a 6 day period between May 28
and The first 22 ft of hole was drilled on May 28th using the
Barth Drill Model 150 which was the same rig used to drill CH 2 The
remaining 18 ft of the calyx hole was excavated with a Watson 3000 drilling
rig The Watson 3000 is a truck mounted rig with pull downs and is capable of
applying a down pressure of 25 tons From 0 to CH 2 was excavated
with the 42 in diameter drilling bucket Below the hole was
completed using the bucket along with a 42 in diameter core barrel and 24 and
L 4
GEOLABS HAWAII
teeth spaced 10 inches apart around the perimeter of the casing The
teeth were flush with the edge and welded to the side of the casing
A total of 5 working days were required to drill the 40 foot deep
inspection hole The first 22 feet was drilled in one working day The
average rate of excavation from 0 to 9 ft was 17 ft operating hour and from
9 to 19 feet was operating hour
Drilling of the inspection hole in the alluvium sandy silt and
silty fine sand with cobbles weathered tuff clayey silt with gravel
and cinders sand was relatively easy The bucket rig had no difficulty
drilling through the materials and the sides of the large diameter hole
were very stable Between 7 and to 2 ft thick cobble layer
slowed down the advance
From 19 4 to 40 feet interbedded basalt lava flows weathered
tuff silty clay with abundant boulders and bouldery marine sediments
were encountered The equipments used have great difficulties in
excavating the ground From 19 4 to 40 feet the average rate of exca
vation was approximately 3 7 feet per hour The 36 inch long 42 inch
diameter core barrel and the augers used failed to bring up boulders and
_ cored blocks of basalt Hand mining of large blocks from the hole slightly
alleviated the problem but the rate of hole advance was significantly
improved only when a specially made 6 foot long 42 inch diameter core
barrel was used in conjunction with the augers The longer core barrel
allowed the larger blocks of excavated materials to be tightly packed
inside the barrel and then pulled out of the hole Water was added to
the cuttings during drilling to provide some apparent cohesion to the silty
and sandy cuttings and to cool the carbide cutting teeth
In the cinder and clinker zone near the bottom of the inspection
hole overbreak was typically 2 to 3 ft beyond the neat line Other
overbreak zones were typically on top of or in the lava flows
L 5
GEOLABS HAWAII
L 3 2 Casing Installation
On six days after the start of drilling a
excavated hole Installation of the casing was smooth and encountered
no difficulty The sides of the calyx hole was very stable above the
water table except in broken zones within the basalt lava flow at 36 0
to 40 feet where minor sloughing occurred
